0316666343|3.0|After reading the reviews accompanying the book's release, I was very excited, expecting to find \\"These Lovely Bones\\" not only entertaining, but a good literary novel. While certain aspects were well done -- Sebold is both a reasonably good writer and writes creatively and compellingly about Susie's experiences in heaven -- the book succeeds only for the first half.<br />After setting up the characters and their relationships, Sebold apparently doesn't know what to do with them. While the point of the book is the characters' growth in the wake of the tragedy that begins the book, the story peaks and then falters, unable to progress past the arc toward an effective resolution of both themes and plot. The ending is appalling. Even apart from the horrific \\"Ghost\\"-like chapter near the end, the book's \\"happily ever after\\" ending feels false, especially when compared to the refreshing lack of formula employed in the set-up. If only the creativity and authenticity of the first half could have been sustained, what a novel it would have been. I remain hopeful that Sebold may have such a novel in her. Maybe next time.

B00005JKLZ|5.0|I have seen this film get a bad knock or two for literary license. Let us remember that this film was directed by Simon Wells. The original book was written by H.G. Wells. The Director is the great grandson of the famous author. Therefore, can his vision of the Time Machine be any less creative than the original vision of his great grandfather? Both men created a Science Fiction classic presented through their talent and creative presentation of this concept of travel through time. Both ought to be measured on their own merit individually, and both are quite excellent.<br />Comparisons to the original movie, directed by George Pal and starring Rod Taylor, often discount the simple fact that film artists of the time were constrained by the technology available. It was the vision of a gifted director, and certainly a classic and wonderful adaptation of the H.G. Wells novel. Nevertheless, being different and unique, the new version is beautiful and brilliant.<br />Guy Pearce does a fine job in the lead role of distracted scientist, driven by creative passion to create a machine with which to travel into the past to save his fiance. Finding he could not change the past, seeks to find answers in the future that will enable him to ultimately save her.<br />The special effects sequences are astounding and altogether marvelous. The time machine itself is a construct of pure genius. It is beautiful, brilliant, and astounding in design. The effects seen as centuries pass and the world changes around the time traveller are some of the best special effects ever produced.<br />The purpose of movies is to entertain, and this one delivers in abundance. It is not a remake of George Pal's vision, nor does it conform entirely to the H.G. Wells novel. It is a unique vision of Simon Wells, and viewed on its own merit, is absolutely excellent. The purpose of Science Fiction is to inspire a certain degree of awe and sense of wonder in boldly charging into the unknown. This film does this as deftly as any made in recent years.<br />My only reservation is that English would still be a living language 800,000 years from now. That struck me as highly unlikely. So too the lack of weathering on stones with carved lettering from centuries past. Aside from that small gripe, I found the film to be amazing and wonderful. I was entirely entertained.<br />I give this film 5 stars, and expect to watch it repeatedly over the years and enjoy it many times. It is one of the few made in recent times that one wishes to see again and again. In fact, after seeing it in the theatre twice, I have seen it on DVD 3 times already! 1573220485|5.0|Welcome to Utopia. Alex Garland's debut is one of the best travel writings I've ever read. His writings literally brings you to the streets of Bangkok and the immaculate beaches of Thailand.  The characters in the book, particularly Richard and Jed (which incidentally, was apparently butchered by the screenwriter and therefore totally absent from the movie), are distinctly memorable. One may also call this book an up-to-date, hip, BazLuhrmanized version of Lord Of The Flies. <br />It's not so much about backpacking, smoking...or a prolonged summer vacation. The lovers' long-awaited kiss at the end of the film seems timid and dull after what we've been exposed to earlier in the film.  This isn't a moral criticism...it's an artistic one. 0345446798|5.0|My library of dog books is growing steadily, and this book is the latest addition. It is also the only one that I have read from cover to cover. Unlike other dog training books that I have read, this book does not give you step by step instructions on how to solve certain dog behavioural issues. Instead, Dr. McConnell, in fluid and conversational prose, reveals to readers what years of research and experience have taught her about dog behaviours. She draws parallels between dogs and humans, and also demonstrates, through stories, how humans often mis-read and mis-communicate with our doggie partners. The real-life stories are sometimes funny, sometimes bittersweet, and sometimes downright sad, but they all serve a purpose - to teach us why dogs behave the ways they do, and how us humans can improve and enhance our relationships with our canine friends by having a better and deeper understanding of how they communicate. An excellent read and certainly a critical addition to a dog-lover's library.

B000066I6N|5.0|Great album, one of the best from Plant's solo repertoire. The album is more blues oriented than we've heard from him since early Zep. I remember years ago reading a review of a Rolling Stones album where the reviewer criticized one song by derisively calling it &quot;painfully slow blues&quot;. I thought, &quot;but I LIKE painfully slow blues!&quot;. Well, my favorite on this album is a cover of Jesse Colin Young's &quot;Darkness, Darkness&quot;, and if anything deserves to be described as painfully slow blues, it is this. Another highlight is Skip Spence's &quot;Skip's Song&quot;, I wasn't familiar with it but it features a beautiful melody that moves in totally unpredictable yet wonderful directions. Great cover of Dylan's &quot;One More Cup Of Coffee&quot;, with a lovely flamenco acoustic lead guitar. I like the fact that all the songs he covers that I was familiar with from other versions sound totally different here. 0962477060|5.0|I came to this book as a 20-something with her first guitar as a recent birthday gift. I had experience playing the piano, but nothing too impressive, got into some intermediate theory. My experience with the violin was equally unimpressive (and far more punishing for anyone in earshot). After a while of playing around, and after the fun of songbooks by my favourite artists had worn off, I wanted to get started for real. My main problem was that I was sure the guitar must make *sense* somehow--I'm no good at math or physics, but even I knew the spacing of the strings had to lead to a system. But what was it?<br />Thank goodness I found this book before things got so infuriating I quit. I wonder for how many people that's happened. Bill Edwards presents the fretboard as something that, purposely or miraculously, makes sense. Understanding the system prevents you from getting lost or feeling that everything you do right is luck; it allows you to manipulate the guitar. I bought the Special Edition (Fretboard Logic SE), and I would definitely recommend anyone else doing the same. The first book is good, but you'll want more, and I think that on it's own its not enough. I am now on the third book, which is also very helpful.<br />Make no mistakes, this book isn't a gimmick. You won't be a singer/songwriter by the end, you won't have a catalogue of songs, you won't speak french in 30 days, shed 90% of your body fat, lose 10 years of wrinkles and blemishes... This book just shows you how to make sense of the fretboard, and it is the most effective way I've experienced or heard of (I had two other popular guitar books, and have put up with endless stories of friends' failed guitar lessons). It will help you in leaps and bounds, but nothing can *make* you a guitar player.<br />The book is surprisingly user-friendly, though I did have to go back and re-read things, or give it a day and try again later. I do have issues with how he presented augmented forms and some of the sevenths in Book Two (I've noticed misprints and he could've been clearer about where the point of reference--not always the barre--is located in relation to the \\"full forms\\"). Don't get too discouraged if it gets laborious or temporarily beyond you. It's a lot to absorb and understand! It does take work, but you'll be rewarded infinitely, because the guitar begins to open up instead of being a sterile tool upon which you can whip out some memorised cords or riffs. I wholeheartedly recommend this book, and maybe some songbooks so you can experience the pleasure of playing \\"actual songs\\" that you will recognise and enjoy. <br />Before you can do anything you dream of, or anything meaningful at all, you have to understand the guitar. This helps. Now go practise.

B000069HKH|5.0|Bruce Springsteen is without question a songwriter of immense talent and overwhelming emotion. He puts all of his talent to work on this amazing album, finds talent he has not shown before, and gives us a touchstone to remind us of the pain that was September 11th; one that will stand as a testament to those who died, and as hope for all of us. That spirit of unity pervades this fine album, each track another facet of our collective experience, each lyrical passage a complete portrait in itself.<br />Springsteen reunites with the E Street Band on this album, and it couldn't have been any other way. Connecting all of the emotions and stories that came out of Sept. 11th took all of their prodigious talents, and the connection and understanding of each other's abilities pushes Springsteen's phenomenal songs to an even higher place.<br />Every song has at least one perfect passage, one shining moment that moves the deepest part of your soul. In a time when whole albums pass by without a hint of that kind of connection, and in a time when we need that connection more than ever, Springsteen has delivered what has to be considered one of the finest American musical achievements ever. He captures the pain of the lost in the track &quot;You're Missing,&quot; the courage of the fire fighters and police in &quot;Into the Fire,&quot; the emptiness of the survivors in &quot;Nothing Man,&quot; and finds hope in every song, even if it is hidden and almost impossible to hold on to.<br />Capturing the spirit of the living, honoring the memory of the dead, or shining a light of hope through the darkness of grief would be an accomplishment of artistic triumph. Achieving all three in one recording, at times in one song, in one passage, is a miracle.<br />In &quot;Countin' on a Miracle,&quot; &quot;There ain't no storybook story/ There's no never-ending song/ Our happily ever after Darlin'/ forever come and gone/ I'm movin' on/ If I'm gonna believe/ I'll put my faith/ Darlin' in you.&quot; The Boss has it all, lost dreams, recaptured hope, and the resolve to move on. It is nearly perfect.

B0000025D0|5.0|'Darkness On The Edge of Town,' stark, staring, and pure to its core, stands as one of the defining moments of rock 'n roll.<br /><br />Charting a course of earnest youthful rebellion through a barren, stripped-away landscape of working class frustration and hopelessness, the album's protagonist repeatedly strikes out to forcibly carve his own opportunities for freedom, salvation, belief, and transcendence. These themes, the distilled essences of rock 'n roll, have been written into song thousands of times before 1978 and since, but few rock 'n roll performers have ever achieved quite as singular and powerful a vision as Springsteen does here.<br /><br />Presenting a far less enthusiastic persona that he did on the previous 'Born To Run' (1975), this album finds the Springsteen character past the initial golden rush of youth, thrills, and new frontiers and abruptly confronted suddenly with the unyielding face of reality's harsh and sobering truths.<br /><br />Writing with a sharp insight and wisdom he would later largely relinquish, Springsteen tackles, with a good view from the wrong side of the tracks, the inevitable toil, toll, and hardships of life, the inexorable and corroding passage of time, the dark psychology of the unexamined life, and, significantly, the problems of the father, but not those of the mother, who, unaddressed, quietly suffuses the album and may lie behind most of its presented struggles.<br /><br />Springsteen is smart enough throughout to acknowledge his own culpability, and on 'Prove It All Night,' sings of the thrill of transgression, stealing, cheating and lying, something Springsteen, standing in proud judgement like the king of the mountain, believes the square 'suits' in the good part of town will never know.<br /><br />Finding precious relief and moments of salvation in lust, the pursuit of love and women, drag racing, acts of defiance, and the night itself, Springsteen achieves a pure daemonic epiphany in \\"Streets of Fire,\\" the record's black center.<br /><br />Having abandoned life, society, and himself in a frenzy of spiritual destitution and derelict intensity, the singer, \\"a loser down these tracks,\\" remembers a nighttime walk to the place he used to drag race with others, and 'in the darkness\\" hears somebody call his name. But this is very likely not the voice of another person, but a vision, delusion, or transcendent force: this is a confrontation and communion, Springsteen's dark night of the soul. Does he answer? If so, what occurs? The listener is never told. In the aftermath of the experience he is doubly disenfranchised, \\"talking only to strangers,\\" walking \\"with angels who have no place.\\"<br /><br />In spasmodic rock orgasm 'Candy's Room,' a more confident Springsteen actively pursues another epiphany in the mythologically-imagined Candy, half high school virgin, half New Jersey moll and sad-eyed Mater Dolorosa who \\"can make men do anything she wants.\\" Our hero travels down the vaginal darkness of 'Candy's hall' as if through a gauntlet to her bedroom, where, surrounded by \\"pictures of her heroes,\\" he is confident that \\"what she wants is..me.\\" Numinous Candy, all-encompassing, takes eager control, and lusty Springsteen is blissfully absorbed.<br /><br />The album moves easily between rousing and deeply-felt anthems of will and determination, apocalyptic visions and revenge fantasies (Springsteen imagines a phallic tornado--his 'size does matter' proxy--descending from the sky and destroying the town), thoughtful ruminations, and poignant songs of quiet acceptance concerning life's inherent sadness and tragedies, such as 'Racing In The Streets' and 'The Working Life.'<br /><br />But 'Darkness On The Edge Of Town' is also a grueling document of an isolated loner's counter-projections towards society, one who has an equal potential for bias, assumption, violence, and inaccuracy.<br /><br />On later albums, Springsteen would lose much of the rigorous self-honesty that made 'Darkness onthe Edge of Town' possible.<br /><br />Becoming slowly but increasingly lukewarm and maudlin, the forces he and his characters battle remaining too generalized, unfocused and never exactly targeted, he would devolve into the champion of those who cannot cope, adapt, or evolve through the minimum stages of adult life.<br /><br />In light of the aura of victimization and finger-pointing that clouds his later work, it does not surprise that Springsteen appears on VH1 and other programs today not as a self-fulfilled and self-responsible man who has fought his battles and won, but, thick-set, torpid, and inarticulate, like one of the downtrodden and defeated men of his early lyrics.<br /><br />The great 'rebel heroes' of the 20th century, such as Marlon Brando, Jack Kerouac, Elvis Presley and James Dean, either died young by the code they lived by, or, in middle age, pasty, bloated and gender-erased, become caricatures not of themselves but of their mothers, whose influence they have spent a lifetime running from but have never escaped.<br /><br />Never having specifically located and accepted his own limitations, Springsteen, the fiery yet earthbound romantic, would soon see his problems generating everywhere but at their source, and, blinded, become his own oppressor.<br /><br />The 'Darkness on the Edge of Town' album art bravely portrays a sallow-faced Springsteen, emaciated, hung-over or strung out, perpetually about to go to bed or just waking up.<br /><br />The struggle for clarity, briefly won moment-to-moment here, Springsteen lost with time, as his vision expanded laterally but stopped moving forward.<br /><br />It may be that Springsteen, \\"tied and bound\\" by the umbilical cord of his complexes, spent himself early, leaving himself with nothing to produce but retreads of broken ideals.<br /><br />Defiant Springsteen shouts \\"Hey mister, I ain't a boy, I'm a man,\\" on this album; but the protagonist of 'The River' gets his girlfriend pregnant and so figuratively puts his head right in the oven, when unwanted or unplanned pregnancy is something millions of people around the globe cope with without permanent sulking or a sense of fatality.<br /><br />Are these actions and attitudes those of a boy, or of a man? Listeners may forward to the day when a Springsteen character, down on his luck, fired, or laid off, says resourcefully, \\"so I took some computer classes at the community college and eventually found work.\\"<br /><br />But on 'Darkness on the Edge of Town,' like a ten-song elaboration of British folk-singer Ewan MacColl's classic \\"Dirty Old Town,\\"with its themes of youthful innocence, love, disillusionment, anger, and violence, Springsteen, disconsolate idealist, got it absolutely right.the Edge of Town' possible.<br /><br />Becoming slowly but increasingly lukewarm and maudlin, 6304595247|4.0|Sure, there's a lot wrong with Peter Kosminky's 1992 take on the Emily Bronte classic. For starters, Binoche and Fiennes are both too old for their teenage parts in the first half of the film, and then Fiennes and Janet McTeer(as longtime and long suffering Earnshaw servant Nelly Dean) don't age nearly enough in the second. Even more of a problem is the stunt casting of Binoche - her occasional slight French accent is a trifle inexelicable here, as others have noted - as both Cathy and her honey blonde daughter, Catherine Linton, neither of whom ever left Yorkshire. The script makes some wacky changes, also - instead of Cathy and Heathcliff spying on a lavish ball at the Linton's, they watch Edgar and Isabella play a game of indoor badminton. (O-kaaaaaay....Linton, badminton...well, it sort of rhymes...)<BR> <BR>Instead of focusing on the minuses, I prefer to admire this adaptation's stong points. Unlike the mushy tho' oddly revered 1939 version (and the 1970 one with Timothy Dalton), it tells the entire story, which despite everyone's fond memories, isn't a romance so much as it is a revenge tale and one of the first complex psychological portaits of an abused boy becoming a brutal, abusive man. To that end, Ralph Fiennes is a note-perfect Heathcliff; the scene of his humilitation of Isabella is right on the money. (One feels that he'd eat Olivier's wimp of a Heathcliff for tea, after making him clean the stables three times over.) Despite her miscasting, Binoche makes a lovely, haunting Cathy in the first half and a sympathetic pawn in Heathcliff's twisted plan in the second. Add to that fine support from McTeer, Simon Shepherd (Edgar Linton), Sophie Ward (Isabella) and a young Jeremy Northam (Hindley); heck, even Sinead O'Connor, with her eyes as wide as saucers, catches the right Bronte groove as narrator Emily herself. (What would O'Connor have been like as Cathy, one wonders...) Again, unlike the 1939 flick, Kosminsky sets the story in its proper time period (1770s - very early 1800s), with superb costumes and production values. Authentic Yorkshire moors and other locations add to the tragedy's flavour. The frosting on the cake, though, is Ryuichi Sakamoto's shattering score, which conveys the story's haunting wildness in one swelling phrase almost better than the entire film does itself. (Pity that - like the movie - it was never released in the U.S.; there's a suite arrangement on Sakamoto's CINEMAGE album that merely leaves you wanting the whole score, available only as a rare and pricey import from Japan.) Perhaps a DVD edition, if ever produced, will contain the score, as well as scenes chopped from the truncated print run on Bravo. Until a better version comes along (the 1998 MASTERPIECE THEATRE one with Orla Brady and Robert Cavanah had a few great gothic bits - Heathcliff digging up and sobbingly embracing Cathy's bones really hit the core of the tale), the Fiennes and Binoche take will remain my favourite visit to WUTHERING HEIGHTS. It's a bumpy ride all right, but well worth the trip.
0805066691|5.0|Americans have always studied the American Indian from a viewpoint looking Westward -- this is a book that looks at them, looking Eastward. New perspectives (at least new when the book was published in 1970) and shocking statistics changed our view of Indian-White relations in the 19th Century. Characters speak for themselves in quotes from Presidents, Chiefs, generals, braves and cavalry troops.<br />In one chapter the reader is invited to a Cheyenne winter camp to observe their preparation for the hard season. Then, before dawn the next morning, we are led to watch in horror as Lt. Col. George Custer leads a cavalry charge on the village. Of course there is nothing here of the actions that prompted such malevolent response, but that would not serve the purpose of the book. For the purpose of this book is totally one-sided -- to portray one side of the American Indian experience and to cast them in a much more sympathetic light had been done in previous writings. The book is pure, narrowly-focused historic revisionism with no pretense toward balance.<br />All that said, it's a valuable book because for us to understand our heritage, we need to know about and be sympathetic to, the truth of our history, whether it strikes us as &quot;good&quot; or &quot;bad.&quot; What looks bad to us in the 21st Century may not have looked that way to us had we lived in the 19th and known only what they knew.<br />This book gives us some of the truth and it should be read and appreciated for what it is rather than lauded as the final word on Indian-White political relations. Let's hope that someday someone will have the courage to write &quot;the other side of the story.&quot; And, folks, there IS another side that is equally poignant and equally valuable.

Jackson, whose knowledge of the city works like a charm both in terms of the plot and the film's entertainment value.  <br />Speaking of Willis and Jackson, DHWV is played as a buddy show, and the chemistry between the two is simply excellent, easily equalling anything Mel Gibson and Danny Glover have worked on together.  Jackson, as a new addition to the series, plays a near-racist black man who initially dislikes McClane, but eventually becomes friends with. Willis' and Jackson's verbal spars strike up some big laughs, and better yet, you actually care about their fates, not only as to whether or not they're going to live through the situation, but the fact that you hope they'll get past their differences and end up as best buddies.  Sure, it's all predictable, but it's all played out with so much fun, you don't even really care.  Also worth mentioning among the cast is a German-accented Jeremy Irons as the bomber, this being his last truly excellent villainous performance before he'd go on to embarrass himself in Dungeons and Dragons.<br />The action's great and non-stop, with the expected explosions, shootouts, and fist fights, as well as several superb car chases, including a drive through a park and a tidal wave of water in a tunnel that need to be seen to be believed.  Getting a breather is a little unlikely, but it's nice to see a film that manages to develop its lead characters nicely in the midst of so much action.  If the film disappoints in any aspect, it's with several leaps of logic in the plot (they're very noticeable, too) and the conclusion, which feels pretty limp in comparison to all the exciting material that came before it.  Still, that's only about five minutes out of a two-hour thrill ride that engages the nerves and the brain.

B000069HKH|5.0|You know what?    You probably won't know what to make of this album the first time you listen to it. Bruce isn't really breaking new ground, but ... it's definitely different. My first thought was .. why so much repetition in the verses?  Why include fun, bouncy songs amid the darkness?  <br />I listened again. I smiled. After reading some interviews he'd done, I knew what he was attempting to do.  The reason for the repetition is that .. these are, essentially, hymns.  It's the same call and response delivery that preachers use.   In other words, to drive home a point .. a FEELING.. you want to almost invoke a kind of meditative state.  The subject matter has its footing in the 9/11 tragedy, but it's not an album ABOUT 9/11.  It's simply that .. he's a songwriter and .. he felt a duty to describe the emotion that was/is in the air.   He was moved to write by a fan who pulled up alongside him, saying .. &quot;We need you NOW&quot;.  What followed just poured out from his soul...<br />The songs?  It's good stuff.  From the blues of &quot;Empty Sky&quot; to the wonderful &quot;Paradise&quot;, he covers every emotion here. I like the melody of &quot;Nothing Man&quot;, the church choir of &quot;My City of Ruins&quot; and .. well.. all of it.  The funny thing is, probably my favorite track is the most lightweight of them all - &quot;Let's Be Friends&quot;.   It might have been a &quot;throwaway&quot; on an earlier Springsteen album, but.. it's needed here.   The fun songs are melodic medicine to help bring you to that &quot;sunny day&quot;.  <br />There is a definite spiritual aura to these songs.  Bruce has been &quot;playing&quot; at the preacher role in recent concerts, but I think he now realizes that he is one of the few - the absolute few - who has earned our trust. We believe in his message.  He has always been about hope and inspiration, but... this is the first time he's literally trying to help us rise up.<br />We don't need to hear about the cool Romeos and the Tom Joads. We need to believe again - to find the strength to do all the little things: to travel, to hope, to love.  I was one of the fans praying for a return to the &quot;early music&quot; of '73-'75 - a romantic period with some of the most beautiful poetry.  That, or .. I was hoping for a return to &quot;Nebraska&quot; - an underrated gem of a record. I lost track of the fact that .. Bruce is 52yrs old.  He's a grown man with a family, with children.  His catalog is a cycle of life - from the romantic street poet to the brooding young man, from folk poet to rock superstar.  Finally, he's now become one of a small, rarified group of spokesmen ... not for a &quot;generation&quot;, but for all of us.  He's been writing songs the past decade that chronicle those hiding in the shadows, hurt - the poor in Tom Joad, the AIDS patient in Philadelphia and now ... those we just can't afford to turn a blind eye to - our next door neighbor who might be feeling a tremendous about of grief over the loss of a loved one or .. a man who is tired of hearing how &quot;brave&quot; he was (&quot;Nothing Man&quot;) and has to struggle, nightly, with guilt and a temptation to end it all - to quiet the noise.  <br />It's a wake up call.  He's always championed the common man - the hero in us all.  <br />The fascinating thing about Springsteen is that .. he rescued rock from disco in the mid 70's. He brought integrity back to rock in the glitsy 80's.  Now.. at a time when we're all just trying to find something positive to latch onto - some kind of hope, here comes Bruce again with an album of hymns to fire up that choir, help exorcise our demons and get us to rise again.<br />Welcome back, friend... and.. thanks.<br />I can't wait for the revival to come to town. &quot;Turn it up..&quot;

0609610570|5.0|Execution is a great book!  It is a must read for anyone serious about business.  The authors define execution as follows \\"Execution is a systematic process of rigorously discussing the hows and whats, questioning, tenaciously following through and ensuring accountability.\\"<br />Execution provides a systematic framework for making sure strategy and execution go hand in hand to get results.  The authors describe the three core processes that make up execution as the people process, the strategy process, and the operations process.  Unless there is alignment in these processes there will not be effective execution.  The book goes into a great level of detail on each of these processes and Larry Bossidy provides some great examples of how these processes work at GE and Honeywell.<br />To prove their case the authors show a couple of very high profile execution success and failures.  In light of many high level business failures recently it seems clear that these companies did not have a culture of execution nor were their top executives involved in how the company actually ran.<br />The bottom line is that execution is a great book with great advice on getting things done and establishing accountability for results.  Buy this book!

B000024D4R|5.0|I have always thought The Wall was one of Pink Floyd's most overrated albums. Funny that I think their previous offering, Animals, is their most underrated. Perhaps the fact, with the exception of the opening and closing acoustic numbers (&quot;Pigs on the Wing Part 1 &amp; 2&quot;), none of the songs are under 10 minutes long, and are better appreciated by prog rock fans used to 10+ minute long epics, which this album is almost entirely comprised of. While Roger Waters was tightening up control of the band by this point, it hasn't yet ruined the band, and it still sounds like a band effort here. I remembered when I was young, I was trying to understand what the meaning of Pigs, Dogs, and Sheep was all about, why would such a band be writting songs about barnyard animals. Until I found about about George Orwell and his second best known work (after 1984) called Animal Farm, and now the lyrics to the album makes perfect sense. The &quot;sheep&quot; are mindlessly obediant followers, the &quot;pigs&quot; are the thought police, and the &quot;dogs&quot; are power-mad violent manipulators. So the album is basically taking a slap at the predatory &quot;dog eat dog&quot; capitalist system. Now back to the music, Pink Floyd's role in the progressive rock scene has always been in question, but make no doubt the impact they made in that scene (such little known bands like Hawkwind, Porcupine Tree, Eloy, Pulsar, and many more owe to the likes of Pink Floyd), but I have to say Pink Floyd was progressive and Animals is essentially the last album in that style, as songs like &quot;Dogs&quot;, &quot;Pigs (Three Different Ones)&quot;, and &quot;Sheep&quot; clearly demonstrate. &quot;Pigs (Three Different Ones)&quot; also takes a swipe at Mary Whitehouse, but since I live in America, I know little of her, but she's supposedly the mid 1970s, British equivalent of Tipper Gore, more or less. &quot;Sheep&quot; is definately one of my favorites because I heard this long before getting Animals thanks to my dad owning their A Collection of Great Dance Songs compilation album. This is their only post-Dark Side of the Moon album in the 1970s where there were no radio hits, but that never bothers me, in fact, that's the reason why I like it so much, because no songs on this album had been ran in the dirt by radio airplay. Animals is to me Pink Floyd's last great album, and if you're a fan, get this album.

In fact, this isn't a story that solely relies on plot to move forward, but the rich characters that you come to love. B000065V1V|4.0|Poor Bowie . . . no matter what he does, unless he can create something that can eclipse his early masterpieces like &quot;Low&quot; and &quot;Ziggy Stardust&quot;, people will continue to pan him off as a pseudo-hasbeen. Which is unfair, because this disc proves that he can still make good music without just pickpocketing from his back catalogue or cozying up to the latest mainstream trend. Of course it's not better than Ziggy . . . but it doesn't need to be, because it's good all on its own.<BR>One striking thing about this album is how relaxed Bowie sounds . . . he makes it sound easy after all these years. Especially on his cover of &quot;I Took A Trip On A Gemini Spaceship&quot;.<BR>This album also seems more aware of its surroundings than &quot;hours&quot; . . . the saxophone used on &quot;Gemini Spaceship&quot; spins the tune towards a 50's feel, acknowledging that songs about space are a bit silly (like the flying saucer movies of the 50's) and grounding it in more terrestrial concerns. And there's something delightfully 50's about the doowop background vocals of &quot;Everyone Says Hi&quot; and the chugging bass of &quot;Slow Burn&quot;. Of course, I didn't live through the fifties . . . all I've really got is a celluloid representation of an era, but Bowie seems to relate to time in a similarly stilted fashion, fusing together aspects and whispers from all over the last 50 years to create a timeless sound.<BR>So yeah, this is a good album. I got it because I'm a Bowie completist, but I enjoy it for it's own merrits, and when I caught myself singing &quot;A Better Future&quot; in my head the next week, I knew there was something to it.
0446677450|5.0|Robert Kiyosaki had the good fortune of having two dads: his biological father and his best friend's father, both of whom had opposing views on finances and building a financial future. His \\"rich dad\\" went on to build a billion dollar empire. His poor dad rose to the top of his profession, yet died poor.<br />Kiyosaki pulls no punches when he delivers hard-hitting financial advice that he learned over the years from his rich dad. These aren't \\"get rich\\" or \\"make lots of money easily\\" tips, but rather a system that takes careful planning and tough choices but, if followed, will change your life.<br />keep in mind that this book is a primer for investment strategy.  Kiyosaki does not lay out a step-by-step plan for accumulating wealth, but rather focuses on building the right foundation on which you will build your own wealth.  Contrary to what other reviewers have said, Kiyosaki does not encourage people to quit their jobs and not go to college.  He actually encourages both -- explaining that experience and knowledge are important.  Kiyosaki spends a great deal of time insisting that individuals take classes, read books and use tax/legal advisors to help build their empires from the ground up.<br />With that said, I've spent years studying investing strategies and the ins and outs of building companies so that I could make a move into self-employment and eventually financial independence. My drive to not waste my life making other people rich has been something that's been at odds with my own father's philosophy and that of most people I've known. Kiyosaki says it is this very attitude that seperates the rich from the poor and he takes his time explaining how. The knowledge I've gained over the years in investing and company-building now has a new perspective: Kiyosaki teaches you not to work for yourself, but rather have your money work for you and make you rich by acquiring assets rather than liabilities (sounds simple, but I was surprised by how many people don't understand the difference between the two).<br />There are a lot of books out there that try to teach you to be rich through being cheap, or how to make money quickly by day-trading or just about anything else. THIS IS NOT ONE OF THEM.  Kiyosaki will help you build a foundation on which your fortune can be built, but it takes time and commitment on your part.  Most people are turned off by that concept and will spend the next 20 years trying to find a get-rich-quick plan, rather than just gradually building up their empire over that time span.<br />Kiyosaki concedes that you can save your way to wealth, but why? What's the point of spending years building financial independence and not enjoy the fruits of your labor? At the same time, Kiyosaki's strategies teach a healthy mix of self-control and wise-investing.<br />This book isn't for everyone. If you are willing to learn a new way of thinking and are willing to take some risk and serious commitment to your dream in order to meet your financial dreams, then this book is for you.<br />If you want to play it safe and go the \\"tried and true\\" route, then don't waste your time on this book.  The \\"tried and true\\" route is the one that keeps everyone else in debt and away from their financial dreams.  If that's your way of thinking, I'd recommend saving your money on this book and putting it toward your retirement fund.understand the difference between the two).<br />There are a lot of books out there that try to teach you to be rich through being cheap, or how to make money quickly by day-trading or just about anything else. THIS IS NOT ONE OF THEM.  Kiyosaki will help you build a foundation on which your fortune can be built, but it takes time and commitment on your part.  Most people are turned off by that concept and will spend the next 20 years trying to find a get-rich-quick plan, rather than just gradually building up their empire over that time span.<br />Kiyosaki concedes that you can save your way to wealth, but why? What's the point of spending years building financial independence and not enjoy the fruits of your labor? At the same time, Kiyosaki's strategies teach a healthy mix of self-control and wise-investing.<br />This book isn't for everyone. If you are willing to learn a new way of thinking and are willing to take some risk and serious commitment to your dream in order to meet your financial dreams, then this book is for you. <br />If you want to play it safe and go the \\"tried and true\\" route, then don't waste your time on this book. B000003S09|5.0|If any album foreshadowed the end of the 1960s, it would be this one. This album is filled with morbid themes and unsettling rhythms and melodies. Their experiments in jazz, classical, and heavy rock was like the exact opposite of all things psychedelic. They avoided pop at all costs, which was extremely admirable.<BR><BR>What makes this debut so challenging is the length and structure of the songs. This album only has 5 songs, the shortest one 6 minutes (the mellow &quot;I Talk To The Wind&quot;). Most debuts have more of a pop sensibility to them and sometimes 6 minutes is the longest song on the album, but not this. Plus, the energy and the somber and angry attitude of the songs was rather daring.<BR><BR>The collapse of the 60s is expressed in the opener &quot;21st Century Schizoid Man,&quot; with its lyrics about war and greed, and a very chaotic middle 6/8 jazz-secion, and of course one of the heaviest riffs ever. War is also expressed in a more medieval way with the closing title track, with the use of woodwinds, and the mellotron. Depression and confusion makes up &quot;Epitaph,&quot; one of the saddest songs I can think of, and then &quot;Moonchild&quot; which deals with some sort of love. It has  a beautiful guitar line to it, before it goes into about 10 minutes of soft jazz guitar noodling. &quot;I Talk to the Wind,&quot; I think deals with the idea of poor communication between people, referring to humans as &quot;the wind&quot; for &quot;the wind cannot hear.&quot;  if you look at the lyrics closely.<BR><BR>A great debut by one of the most groundbreaking bands in history.<BR>King Crimson and John Lennon's solo debuts (though released a year after this one) was all the proof a person needed to say that the mentality of the 1960s was over.
B00006AUIG|3.0|Session Nine really left me scratching my head.  To be fair, the first time I watched it the conditions were somewhat shy of optimal.  I broke it up over two days and was pulled away during several key sequences by phone calls and babies crying.  To make sure that I gave the film it's proper attention I locked all the doors and watched it through a second time, but was still left with most of the same questions.  <br />To start off, the characters are the basic archetypes that inhabit thrillers such as these.  This being a `working man thriller', the roles do not include one of the most recognizable; the mad (or at least quirky) scientist.  Alien made it without one, so I guess we can survive sans one here.  Present are:<br />The business owner/corporate guy focused on saving the business/profits and damn the rest of the world. (Except, of course, his family back home usually shown through rain swept windows or in a nice soft focus memory)<br />The hard working, morally centered second in command who has recently lost the love of his life to...<br />The lazy bastard everyone loves to hate.  <br />Also present are: The intellectual who doesn't really fit in working with the blue collar guys, but has a secret; and the rookie kid on his first job who we all know will end up dying some horrible way.<br />The first third of the movie is dedicate to the set up and loaded with the required exposition.  I always find it amazing that security guards and DPW workers can be such a thorough source of back story and vital historical information.  The head of the DPW who contracts our heroes to remove the asbestos from the soon to be remodeled asylum is conveniently married to the local historian; a slightly contrived convenience, but forgivable since the device does allow the story to move along quicker.<br />Looking back,  I see that most of what I've stated thus far has been pretty harsh, but I did like this flick.  The characters, though broadly sketched, are likeable (well,except for the lazy bastard) and the acting made me forgive most of the weaknesses of the script.  What really pulled me out of the experience, even in the second viewing, was the \\"twist\\" ending.<br />The way the clues are laid out as to who the killer is really do point to pretty much any one of the guys working (young guy who you know will die horribly excluded, of course) and a few other folks as well.  What it reminded me of, strangely enough, was a stage play my wife and I saw in Boston several years ago called Shear Madness.  The plot revolves around a murder in a hair salon and at the end the audience actually votes to decide who the killer is.  There are enough `facts' pointing at each cast member that when the detective starts explaining the motives and opportunities we can believe the person we selected is guilty.  Session 9 could easily subject itself to such a gimmick.  (Hmm.  With DVD's branching abilities...)<br />I don't know if this is supposed to be a puzzle movie, like the Sixth Sense, or a straight on psychological/haunted house horror like The Shining and I'm not convinced that the movie itself knows what it wants to be.  If only the cast weren't so damn likeable it would be easy to pan this flick entirely, but I can't.  David Caruso and company bring such believability and a wonderfully grounded nature to this movie that I really do feel compelled to watch it a third time and really `get' it.  I only hope that there really is something to get.

B00005JKNF|5.0|'Gosford Park' will, unfortunately, not appeal to everyone.  Some will think it moves too slow, some will think there's not enough action, and some will not want to keep up with all the characters.  That's too bad, but for those who stick with it, 'Gosford Park' will be a wonderful movie experience.<br />The plot is simple.  In November, 1932, Sir William McCordle (Michael Gambon) has invited friends and family to his country estate for a hunting weekend.  Everyone arrives with his or her baggage, physical and emotional.  Director Altman expertly allows the viewer to eavesdrop on bits and pieces of seemingly random conversations that serve not so much to give us plot information, but to give us an insight into the characters and their lives.  And to pave the way for murder.<br />The first half of the film is a brilliant look at the rich and their servants.  The attendants are often treated as part of the furniture.  Often the wealthy guests carry on intimate conversations among themselves with the servants clearly in earshot.  But they are servants, their job is to serve, not to report conversations.  But when the rich are alone with their servants, they confide in them and share information they would never share with anyone else.  Altman does a masterful job of showing us that even though this is an English estate from 70 years past, things really haven't changed much.  <br />Many excellent characters are present in the film.  Sir William seems bored with the charade of hosting this event and seems to want to spend time only with his dog and his mistress.  The feelings are mostly mutual.  His dog seems to be the only one present who really loves him.  Bob Balaban is wonderful as American movie director Morris Weissman, who is more concerned about filming his next Charlie Chan movie than the real-life murder right under his nose.   <br />While all these character revelations are important and fascinating, they do go on perhaps a bit longer than they should.  Afterall, the film is past the halfway point before the murder is even committed.  But as the film moves closer towards the conclusion, viewers will begin to piece together all the information presented during the first hour to find a solution.  I would almost say that the solution is not as important as the journey getting there, but it is important because of its power.   <br />In watching the film, I almost thought that the movie did not deserve the R rating.  The rating was put there for some language and some very brief [love] situations, but probably to help define the audience.  Kids would probably not enjoy the film at all.  The same could probably be said for action-crazed teenagers.  However, if you enjoy interesting characters portrayed by a stellar cast in the hands of a masterful director, 'Gosford Park' is for you.  Enjoy.<br />Running time:  2 hours 16 minutes

0553212478|5.0|Those who know Frankenstein only from movies and television may well be surprised to read the original book by Mary Shelley.  Indeed, one may well look back to the cover to see if the book is in fact Frankenstein because the first pages consist of messages from an R. Walton to his sister concerning his expedition to the northern polar regions.  Victor Frankenstein appears as a wretched creature stranded on an ice floe beside the ship.  After he is rescued and recovers somewhat, he tells his incredible story to Walton, who in turn preserves the story in writing.  Frankenstein reminisces about his happy childhood, particularly the close relationship between himself and his &quot;cousin&quot; Elizabeth, and then explains how his interest in discredited natural philosophy led him to create a living man of his own design.  The creature is a hideous, misshapen, giant of a man who so disgusted Frankenstein upon his awakening that he fled his laboratory and residence.  The creation process, it should be noted, in no way involved an elaborate machine powered by lightning such as is portrayed in the movies; in fact, beyond the fact that chemicals are involved, we are told nothing of the process.  For two years, Frankenstein goes about life with a clinging sense of guilt and nervousness, hoping the creature has perished.  When his little brother is murdered, though, he returns home and soon discovers that it was the monster who committed the deed.  In an isolated mountainous area, the monster appears before him and explains his actions.  Although the creature does nothing more than grunt in the movies, the original Frankenstein was possessed of great eloquence and intelligence, and he tells a moving story about his attempts to make a connection with a society that is revolted at the sight of him.  He describes living in a small hovel at the back of a small house for many months, watching the interaction of the family inside, learning how to speak their language and eventually even learning how to read.  When he eventually tries to interact with the family, he is assaulted and runs away, and it is at this point that his virtuous and peaceful nature begins to become inflamed into a torrent of hatred for mankind. When he rescues a girl from drowning, he is rewarded with a bullet in his shoulder. Each time he performs a good deed, mankind runs from him in disgust or attempts to harm him.  Living in total isolation and loneliness, his one purpose in life eventually becomes tormenting his creator, and many terrible misfortunes ensue for Frankenstein, who himself eventually becomes dedicated to the sole purpose of destroying his tormentor.<br />Both Frankenstein and his monster are tragic figures.  The complete alienation of the creature makes him a very sympathetic character in many respects, and he does perform many kind deeds for humans early on.  Frankenstein is an utterly pitiable character utterly destroyed by his mad decision to create the monster; he burdens his soul with responsibility for every crime the monster commits, and his inability to tell anyone his secret for so long destroys him in body, mind, and soul; everyone and everything he cares for is taken from him by his horrible creation.  His tendency to bemoan his fate at every turn of the narrative can get rather tiresome, but one cannot question the depth of his turmoil.  Both creator and created seem to be mirror images of each other in important ways, their fates clearly tied to one another, each soul deserving both blame and pity.  There is much about human nature, both good and bad, revealed in the monster's life as well as Frankenstein's; the novel is a far cry from horror for the sake of horror.  This is actually a very complex, compelling story full of human pathos; it is unfortunate that modern media have turned Frankenstein's creature into a simple, heartless, mentally deficient monster for the sake of scares and laughs.even learning how to read. B000069HKH|5.0|When terrible things strike the lives of everyday people, humanity reacts in a relatively predictable and understandable manner: shock, grief, and sorrow gently ebb into pools of anger, defiance, and resolve. These too evolve endlessly, until we are left with either bitter cynicism or newfound faith and hope. Bruce Springsteen has been a voice for hope and faith in times of struggle for nearly 30 years, and perhaps we need that voice now more than ever.<br />On the morning of September 11, Mr. Springsteen recalls running to a bridge over the Jersey side, the Magic Rat staring into the fires of Jungleland, his city in ruins. One can imagine that he knew even then that eventually, this album was coming.<br />Come it has, and it brings the return of the mighty E Street Band, back with their old swagger, but slightly tempered by age and circumstance. Ultimately these 15 songs are grounded in the spaces that Bruce Springsteen has always known - hope, faith, redemption, defiance against tragedy and picking up the pieces after loss. It occurs to me that we have lost not only the pre-9/11 everything, but also that small window of grace and humanity that shone so brightly in the months following that day. It was as if when those towers fell, so did all of our alienating American tendencies. It seems easy to believe that we as Americans have never felt closer, more united, more as a family living in a place without regard to sex, age, race or nation, than we did on the morning of September 12. In the past months, that too has been lost, and 'The Rising' echoes that loss as much as any other. This is echoed in \\"Nothing Man,\\" a story told from the perspective of a rescuer who survived the ordeal intact (physically, at least):<br />\\"Around here, everybody acts the same / Around here, everybody acts like nothing's changed / Friday night, the club meets at Al's barbecue / The sky's still, the same unbelievable blue\\"<br />\\"Into the Fire\\" is an early highlight of this release, withthe powerful voice of one saved by a fireman, only to see him turn and head back into the inferno, towards others in need, towards his own grave.<br />\\"May your strength give us strength / may your faith give us faith / may your hope give us hope / may your love bring us love\\"<br />This very simple anthem somehow works, so much so that it may well be the emotional centerpiece of an album soaked with the blood of human loss. Other pieces explore the uncertain journeys many found themselves on in the late autumn of 2001. In \\"Empty Sky\\" Mr. Springsteen seems to be on that sojourn, spinning through spaces where loss and longing dance with yearnings for revenge:<br />\\"I woke up this morning / I could barely breathe / Just an empty impression / In the bed where you used to be / I want a kiss from your lips / I want an eye for an eye\\"<br />The image of a river has flowed through many of Mr. Springsteen's finest works throughout the years, and it has served as a reminder that life itself is fluid. The river is always there to carry us towards a new life of hope and redemption. Its' waters are ever-present to cleanse us, it's life inherent to our sustanence. Now we find the sky above us as a new symbol for life and grief, permeating through \\"The Rising\\" not only as it's own entity, but also for the missing pieces of a city that used to reach skyward. On the title track he sings:<br />\\"Sky of memory and shadow / Your burnin' wind fills my arms tonight / Sky of longing and emptiness / Sky of fullness, sky of blessed life / Come on up for the rising tonight\\"<br />These are, without question, E Street songs, but like every American, Mr. Springsteen and his stalwart band are different beings now. The rollicking \\"Waitin' on a Sunny Day\\" recalls the old joy and romp of the classic rockabilly anthem \\"Out in the Street\\" from 1980's double LP The River, but one senses something held back. The pure, unbridled joy, the freedom, the innocence, is gone. When Mr. Springsteen sings \\"everything will be okay,\\" we believe him, but this the hope found rising from tragedy, and that has a very different flavor.<br />This is an elegy for American innocence. <br />But while that innocence may be gone as assuredly as the 3,000 lives lost that sunny September morning, Mr. Springsteen has once again reminded us that from the darkest pits of our despair, hope still shines. There was, is, and will always be the chance to build again, to rise up with the help of our neighbors, and stand tall. Mr. Springsteen cemented his role as America's musical poet-laureate when he opened the \\"Tribute to Heroes\\" telethon with a soul-grippingly powerful performance of \\"My City of Ruins.\\" He took us into his arms at that moment and gave us hope, a clean and shining moment of light when our world was one of darkness. Pete Townsend once referred to Bruce Springsteen as \\"our fearless leader.\\"<br />Indeed, and thankfully so.

He was also the most consistent of the ex-Beatles with his solo albums containing very little in the way of filler and many quality tracks per album. This selection, however is something of an oddity. George's work can be split into three clear sections, and this album straddles them awkwardly.<br />His work with the Beatles is great, but couldn't we have made a whole disc of this? Where is the ecstatic Blue Jay Way? Where is Old Brown Shoe? Where are his great vocal covers such as Everybody's Trying To Be My Baby? And even George's guitar solos could have been showcased such as his last Beatles work on Free As A Bird. Having said that, Something, Here Comes the Sun, and While My Guitar Gently Weeps are songs that made such an impact they have a life outside the context of the Beatles and really give the listener the idea of Harrison the songwriter developing his art.<br />The second phase of Harrison's work was with his Apple albums. As we might expect, My Sweet Lord is here, but only one track each from Living in the Material World and Extra Texture.<br />The third phase is George's Dark Horse releases and the gravest omissions here come from this time. Blow Away, Got My Mind Set On You, Lennon tribute  All Those Years Ago and Beatles lampoon When We Was Fab are all notable by their absence.<br />OK, the compilers seem to be spoiled for choice, and everything here is great, but perhaps it isn't an overstatement to say that an artist as prolific as George deserved a two or even three disc set. And it isn't really excusable to leave out a number two hit single (Got My Mind Set On You).<br />So, 5/5 for the material, 3/5 for the selection, averaging out at 4/5.

0451523938|5.0|It seems curious that the story of Dr. Jekyll and Mr. Hyde should still hold such a fascination for modern readers. It cannot work very well as a mystery story, since most of us already know the secret of the book, that one character transforms bodily into the other. (Nor is Mr. Hyde a straightforward horror monster - he is capable of vaguely civil conversation.) This short tale unfolds in a somewhat disjointed series of vignettes, as a certain lawyer grasps various strands of the mystery of Dr. Jekyll through chance encounters and legal documents. For all these possible flaws, the concept of the book goes straight to the reader's imagination and also to the conscience. Hyde is the evil within all people, and who among us cannot identify on some level with him, and fear him all the more? Who among us has not in some fashion struggled for our better natures as Jekyll has? The concept is brilliant, and timeless. For this reason, the book remains as absorbing as ever. (Incidentally, the introduction to the \\"mass market paperback\\" edition by Vladimir Nabokov consists largely of a summary of the book's plot, and is thus of limited value.)

So, I was expecting the worst.  I was wrong.<br />As someone completely unfamiliar with the video game on which the film is based, I didn't know what to expect.  In short, Resident Evil is about a group of \\"security\\" personnel who work for a huge conglomerate that is doing all sorts of research - some of it suspect.  One of their more sinister viruses is released and they must go into the secret facility where it is created to try to fix things.  Little do they know that they will be facing all sorts of creepy security devices and ... zombies.<br />Milla Jovovich is the female lead and she manages to carry the film well.  I'm really not that informed on her background, but I will say that while she probably doesn't have a great depth of talent at her disposal, she is a capable actress and I liked her in her role. It was the sci-fi stuff.  The whole zombie aspect seems to be ground better covered by George Romero in Dawn of the Dead.  In fact, the zombie mythos seems to be taken directly from Romero's film:  they move slowly, they look terrible, and they eat flesh of the living.  If you get bitten, scratched, etc. by one of the undead, you, too, will become one of the undead.<br />The sci-fi angle worked better for me (and I don't really like sci-fi). Fortunately, the screenwriters did a very good job of having a few of the characters actually explain the subject to me - and it was done well - as a few of the other characters were designed to be as confused as I was.<br />The script, the sets, the make-up, and the actors made this a very enjoyably scary film. However, as DVD extras become the norm, I think we'll see more and more added features for big budget films and more of the \\"standard fare\\" extras, such as these, offered:  Director and actor commentary, subtitles, music video, biographies of the actors, etc.<br />Have yourself a good scare and watch this film.

B000069HKH|5.0|In his earlier incarnation, Bruce Springsteen believed in the magical power of rock and roll.  The majesty of \\"Born to Run\\" and the epic \\"Born in the U.S.A.\\" served as a reminder of how rock and roll could save mankind, one song at a time.  It was an idealistic belief, full of youthful exuberance, and when that youth grew up, he turned inward, plaintive, and retreated from rock and roll into the hushed tones of \\"Tunnel of Love\\" and \\"The Ghost of Tom Joad.\\"  He delivered expert adult contemporary pop songs and went on tour, but he ceased to be the troubadour of the common man, instead choosing mostly to remain silent.  <br />Until now.  \\"The Rising\\" isn't just the best thing to hit American rock in a decade-it is the triumphant exclamation of the redemptive power of rock and roll, the expression of a faith in rock to transcend a compact disc and change the world.  From its opening, triumphant layered guitar strokes to its gospel-influenced choruses, the album oozes with the self-confidence that comes with being in the business of true rock and roll.  This isn't just an album or a group of songs-for Bruce Springsteen, it's a religious experience, a transformative work of a preacher getting ready to deliver yet another titanic sermon.  Which is exactly what it is.  <br />It is also a statement that requires many, many listens before subtleties begin to emerge.  Obviously, the work is the product of its times, but it is never restricted to those times.  Springsteen is painting wide, broad strokes that will live past memories of September.  He is working in the language of allegory and the vagueness of universality.  If the lyrics aren't as emotive and wrapped with wordplay as they were 25 years ago, they achieve a deeper, more immediate connection through their direct plaintiveness.  The lyrical highlights include the brilliant \\"Nothing Man,\\" \\"Paradise,\\" and \\"You're Missing,\\" the album's three dark, tearful ballads.  <br />The true extraordinary moment, however, comes with \\"Worlds Apart.\\"  Springsteen fuses languages and cultures in a momentous statement of idealism where the worlds of East and West come together in lyric and song.  It is a song, appropriately, about love, companionship, and a desire not to be alone.  The album takes its inspiration not in death but in life.  Musically, the E Streeters are back, although there are less of those distinctively classic E Street moments.  It doesn't matter.  This incarnation of the band can still rock, especially with the reconfigured three guitar line-up, backed by the always powerful Max Weinberg and the beautiful organs and keyboards of Danny Federici and Roy Bittan.  It's also an album of immense range-Springsteen channels American popular music in all its forms, from folk and country to rock and pop, to deliver an eclectic set of tunes, each one unique, different, and beautiful.  <br />In the end, Springsteen uses the language of America in creating an album full of redemptive hope and spirit.  It is a celebration, a celebration of heroism, of love, of sorrow, of sadness, of life itself, in all its complex glory.  It's a clarion call to rise up, to live, to breathe, to love, and to cherish, delivered from a preacher coming back to the pulpit to deliver one more shout to the crowd.  Twenty-five years ago, Bruce Springsteen saved rock and roll.  He just might do it again.

B000002UJQ|5.0|Radiohead's third album sounds better and better as time has past. It has been five years since this band dropped the bombshell on us. This album is as much an album for our times as \\"Abbey Road\\" was for the last generation. Few, if any other albums released in the last 15 years have so defined an era. I'm sure there are those people out there who contest this idea, but for most Radiohead fans, and, I think, serious music listeners, this disc was a revelation.<br />By 1997 we were seeing the wane of 'grunge' and the emergence of 'nu-metal'. The deftones were all over the radio. At least in this respect, the media were promoting harsh styles of music more than ever before. Radio was becoming increasingly homogenized. (You can now go practically anywhere and find a Laser Rock station...grr.) Many music-related magazines and publications were groaning about the death of rock music. OK Computer was released into this discouraging, doubtfull marketplace. The critics raved (most of them). (Early comparisons to Pink Floyd were rampant.) This 'concept' album about the dehumanization of the world, the ascendence of technology to our detriment, becomes a sort of warning call to all of us. Slowly, interest in the band that had been percolating since their magnificent 1994 release The Bends, exploded. Radiohead became one of, if not THE, most famous band in the world. Hailed as the saviours of rock music itself. <br />This disc contains such a dark atmosphere that it is actually enjoyable and depressing at the same time. The weight of heaviness accompanying songs like \\"Exit Music: For A Film\\", \\"Climbing The Walls\\", \\"Lucky\\", and \\"Subterranean Homesick Alien\\" is palpable. But moodiness in itself does not make a great song. Here, the moodiness serves the song and the lyrics-each feeding off the other. The overdriven bass guitar on \\"Exit Music\\" is the musical equivalent of the tense, angst-ridden lyrics. The lyrics of \\"Paranoid Android\\" are non-sensical and the music reflects thisin the way it suddenly changes and meanders-it's like three songs in one.<br />What has changed on this album is that a good deal of the rawness of the previous album has been left behind for the creepy sophistication and coldness necessitated by the lyrics. In this sense, we can easily hear the group moving towards the experimental \\"Kid A\\". Although this disc seems cold and unpassionate, these songs still breathe somehow. This is the magic of OK Computer. Radiohead have managed to tell a story of disfunctionalism, existentialism, and claustrophobia in a way that engages the listener and raises many questions. Listening to this album is also a learning experience.<br />On a final note, kudos must be given to the attention and perfection of the sound itself. Nigel Goodrich and Co. have achieved an amazing diversity of sounds and textures. The drum and bass interplay on the opening track is more than enough reason to pick this up. OK Computer really needs to be enjoyed with headphones. The sounds on the disc are so rich and deep-the sound field is wide and varied. Often Thom Yorke sounds like he is a million miles away, but in the next verse he is sitting right next to you on your couch-or whispering in your ear.
B00004X01U|5.0|Madonna has always been skilled at adapting her music to emerging sounds, taking them to the mainstream in a way that no one else could. On this release, she grasps onto French electro-disco and takes it for a nice ride. The production here is just as integral as the lyrics that are painted across it, the best example being the incredibly infectious &quot;Impressive Instant&quot;, which has a driving beat, and such left of center lyrics as &quot;Astral bodies drip like wine&quot; and &quot;I like to singy singy singy, like a bird on a wingy wingy wingy&quot;. As silly as that does sound, it works incredibly well. The funky clash of the title track made for a perfect intro single, followed on the chart by two totally different sounds, the organic sounding dance pop &quot;Don't Tell Me&quot; and the gritty &quot;What It Feels Like For A Girl&quot;. This album, while being heavily coated in the previously mentioned electro-disco sound, is not content to give into just one sound, as the second single and the track &quot;Gone&quot; both have a much more natural feel to them, and the track &quot;Amazing&quot; is pure pop, a sister to the smash &quot;Beautiful Stranger&quot;. This record manages to keep you enthralled through ten songs, not one of which could be called a dud. She still has the touch.

B000002UDB|5.0|If you read my review of \\"Sgt. Pepper's\\" you may be familiar with the frustration I had in gaining access to my true heart's desire, \\"Abbey Road\\", which I had heard several times in the car (on cassette) on a family vacation to Maine in the summer of 1977.  Shortly thereafter, on one of me and my dad's monthly pilgrimages to the record department at the Korvettes on Staten Island I had to come to grips with the reality of the fact that they were out of \\"Abbey Road\\"...They did have plenty of copies of \\"Magical Mystery Tour\\" and my dad bought that for me instead...I didn't have high hopes because I was completely unfamiliar with the music, but my dad assured me that I would love the album and that it was really good.<br />Did I mention that my dad tended to understate things at times?  I immediately put the record on my turntable and my life completely changed...Prior to that moment I was just an 8 year old kid.  After that moment, I had converted to a card-carrying, confirmed Beatlemaniac.  I didn't know about any of the Beatles history, I didn't know what their discography was comprised of (the extent of my record collection at that point were a couple of Simon & Garfunkel albums, and a bunch of Sesame Street records) and did not know where \\"Magical Mystery Tour\\" stood historically...All I know is that I totally loved the songs.  Like \\"Sgt. Pepper's\\", this album had catchy, sometimes spooky, altogether excellent songs with great and occasionally strange instrumentation.  A perfect album to let an 8-year old's imagination run free.<br />Where to start with favorite songs?  They're all good, except perhaps George Harrison's ode to an L.A. street, \\"Blue Jay Way\\", which is one of the few Beatles songs that have never done it for me...However, the title track just rocked with a great horn section and an ending that kind of just meandered off into someone's imagination (everyone's imagination?)...\\"The Fool On The Hill\\" brought a message that was familiar to an occasionally misunderstood kid...Other favorites is the vaudeville styled \\"Your Mother Should Know\\" which may be seen as schmaltzy, in typical Paul McCartney fashion, but in my eyes is just a really pretty song.  \\"I Am The Walrus\\" is absolutely brilliant nonsense writing with instrumentation and a choral performance that, when combined with the lyrics, is completely spooky - a musical adventure all on its own.  \\"Hello Goodbye\\" is a bit of fluff, but it's pretty good fluff if you ask me.  \\"Strawberry Fields Forever\\" and \\"Penny Lane\\" are evocative odes to Lennon & McCartney's hometown - each have great melodies, the former in a more minor key and psychedelic, the latter using a great soprano trumpeter, interesting lyrics and a major key to paint their stories...I also really love \\"All You Need Is Love\\" for its utopian message and interesting instrumentation and ending.<br />Over the years, this album never lost anything to me...If anything, the lyrics and music took on more complicated meanings...But the album never lost that sense of fun and adventure that it had for me back when I was 8.  And again, this is a strength of this album and something which really makes it special - it is accessible to a kid, but is complex enough to be enjoyed by the adult...It works on various levels...THIS IS A GREAT, GREAT ALBUM - NOT QUITE A MASTERPIECE ON A PAR WITH \\"SGT. PEPPER'S\\" BUT ESSENTIAL NONETHELESS!!!

B000069CWE|5.0|Hybrid Theory was, in the opinion of most, an excellent album, and was certainly a financially successful one, especially considering it was the first serious recording venture made by the California band. The songs were usually bitter, often sorrowful, but seldom really angry in the dark, Nine Inch Nails sense of the word. NIN and bands like it have demonstrated that you don't have to be loud to be bitter and brooding, and Linkin Park has embraced this theory in Reanimation. By and large, the tracks on Reanimation are much quieter than their Hybrid Theory counterparts, but they are much darker. The effect complements the original album very well. The remixes themselves are very masterfully done, most notably 'Enth E End' (the almost jazzy new piano sample will catch your attention right away) and 'Krwlng' (Staind's Aaron Lewis lends himself very well to the song). The remixes of 'Place For My Head' and 'Pushing Me Away'--to which Korn's Jon Davis lends his considerable talents--and the previously unreleased track 'My December' are also well done. However, if you didn't care for Hybrid Theory, don't expect to enjoy Reanimation. Listening to the CD, I found myself preferring and disliking the same songs that I had when I got Hybrid Theory. Overall, though, it's certainly worth your money and an excellent CD to have, especially if it's to complement Hybrid Theory. Just as a brief aside, if nothing else go to a store and look at the back of the CD. You can have a little fun trying to decipher the twisted takes on the names of the original songs. 'Frg/10' gave me fits until I figured it out.

B000069HKH|5.0|The popular music scene has not been much to write home about lately.  There have been a few brief flashes of genius, usually from a previous generation's artist, but that is the extent of music's greatness in this age of mediocrity where every rap and R&amp;B song sounds alike and Limp Bizkit (emphasis on the limp part) rules rock.  Seems that most rock bands have forgotten how to roll anymore.  Re-enter Bruce Springsteen, possibly the greatest American artist of the rock'n'roll era (yes, greater even than Dylan, who I'm a big fan of, but who can't put much emotion into his music).  The Rising is, frankly, the greatest album yet released in this new millenium.  Heck, it's the greatest album released since U2's Achtung Baby back in late 1991.  It's tender, it's sad, AND it rocks AND ROLLS.  Springsteen brings humanity back into music, a device that is absent in the musical meat market of &quot;baby I need you&quot; or &quot;let's bump and grind baby.&quot;  In The Rising, Springsteen fuses loss with hope and humanity with spirituality.  Funny that these items have been gone so long that the ideas sound very original.  Some of these tracks are memorials (Into the Fire, Empty Sky, You're Missing, The Rising, and a few others) but the memorials are not downtrodden eulogies - they bustle with the excitement and heroism of the lives they celebrate and they make the men and women who have given their lives, in Sept. 11 and other events, very real.  Probably the greatest cut on this record is the title track, one of the best songs released in the last decade.  Every song here deserves special consideration.  Mary's Place is a rouser that is akin to Springsteen before Born to Run.  Paradise is a look into a suicide bombers head (empathy is one thing that no artist can handle like the Boss) and a very sad song as well when things do not turn out as promised.  Nothing Man is the story of a survivor of a tragedy - a tragedy that everyone else can get over, but he can't.  Eachsong is infused with energy.  Many of the songs are experimental (well, they're experimental in the sense that Springsteen has never done many of these things before).  Some songs seem fun on the surface, but may have more urgency to them.  Let's Be Friends is an example of this, a hidden desperation that is also found in Dancing in the Dark off of Born in the USA.  All in all, this is a great record and highly recommended.  It's easy to tap your foot to it, but I recommend reading the lyrics along with the lyrics a few times - you'll be glad you did.  73 minutes rarely ever passes by as quickly as when listening to The Rising.  I'm not gonna call it the Boss's best album ever, but I don't really think you can compare his records.  Each of them has a certain sound and spirit that makes them unique and great (except for Human Touch, not a bad record by any means, but not great).  This one is different just like any Boss album - and that's a good thing.  The Rising isn't a Born to Run or Born in the USA redux, it stands on its own.  Thank you Mr. Springsteen for bringing rock'n'roll back!

0553210246|5.0|The Mayor of Casterbridge is undoubtedly one of the finest novels of the Victorian Era - quite a tribute given that the period produced a countless number of classics. The conflict between Michael Henchard and Donald Farfrae reflects a clash between tradition and modernity. This dichotomy is perhaps best encapsulated in their contrasting styles of record keeping; Henchard relies entirely on intuition and memory while Farfrae maintains extraordinarily meticulous accounts of all his transactions. Furthermore the struggle between Farfrae and Henchard reflects a conflict of vastly different temperaments. Henchard proves to be of a passionate temperament while Farfrae is rational and levelheaded. For instance, despite his modest means and limited experience, Farfrae boldly ventures into agricultural trading and through a series of shrewd bets manages to unseat Henchard as Casterbridge's preeminent merchant. In order to regain his erstwhile position, Henchard engages in rash and seemingly desperate speculation - in contrast to the prudent risk taking of Farfrae - that ultimately culminates in his ruin.<br />While some AP students have chastised Henchard for his inability to overcome his animalistic nature, the more astute reader realizes that his inveterate character serves to illustrate a basic theme of most Hardy novels - the philosophy of determinism. The rise and fall of Henchard illustrates the unpleasant reality that one's life is subject to forces beyond one's control - in Henchard's case a deeply flawed character - and these forces often overwhelm the power of one's determination and ambition. This doctrine is certainly anathema to 21st century Americans who firmly believe in the concept of free will and a future holding great promise. While constantly lamenting one's ill fate is self-defeating, it would be naive to entirely disregard the power of destiny to shape one's life for worse as powerfully shown in the tragic tale of the Mayor of Casterbridge.
